</div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Children</th><td class="infobox-data">1</td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><b>Musical career</b></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Genres</th><td class="infobox-data"><div class="hlist hlist-separated"><ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dixieland" title="Dixieland">Dixieland</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Swing_music" title="Swing music">swing</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Traditional_pop" title="Traditional pop">traditional pop</a></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Instrument(s)</th><td class="infobox-data note"><div class="hlist hlist-separated"><ul><li>Vocals</li><li>trumpet</li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><span class="nowrap">Years active</span></th><td class="infobox-data">1919–1971</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2">
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Musical artist</div></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2">Signature</th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Armstrong_signature.svg"><img alt="Louis Armstrong signature.svg" data-file-height="220" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="55" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Louis_Armstrong_signature.svg/150px-Louis_Armstrong_signature.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Louis_Armstrong_signature.svg/225px-Louis_Armstrong_signature.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Louis_Armstrong_signature.svg/300px-Louis_Armstrong_signature.svg.png 2x" width="150"/></a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Louis Daniel Armstrong</b> (August 4, 1901 – July 6, 1971), nicknamed "<b>Satchmo</b>", "<b>Satch</b>", and "<b>Pops</b>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> was an American <a href="/wiki/Trumpet" title="Trumpet">trumpeter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Singing" title="Singing">vocalist</a>. He was among the most influential figures in <a href="/wiki/Jazz" title="Jazz">jazz</a>. His career spanned five decades and several eras in the history of jazz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RCJE_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-RCJE-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>Armstrong was born and raised in <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>. Coming to prominence in the 1920s as an inventive <a href="/wiki/Trumpet" title="Trumpet">trumpet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cornet" title="Cornet">cornet</a> player, Armstrong was a foundational influence in jazz, shifting the focus of the music from collective improvisation to solo performance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Around 1922, he followed his mentor, <a href="/wiki/King_Oliver" title="King Oliver">Joe "King" Oliver</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a> to play in the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Creole_Jazz_Band&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Creole Jazz Band (page does not exist)">Creole Jazz Band</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Creole_Jazz_Band" title="fr:Creole Jazz Band">fr</a>]</span>. In Chicago, he spent time with other popular jazz musicians, reconnecting with his friend <a href="/wiki/Bix_Beiderbecke" title="Bix Beiderbecke">Bix Beiderbecke</a> and spending time with <a href="/wiki/Hoagy_Carmichael" title="Hoagy Carmichael">Hoagy Carmichael</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lil_Hardin_Armstrong" title="Lil Hardin Armstrong">Lil Hardin</a>. He earned a reputation at "<a href="/wiki/Cutting_contest" title="Cutting contest">cutting contests</a>", and his fame reached band leader <a href="/wiki/Fletcher_Henderson" title="Fletcher Henderson">Fletcher Henderson</a>. Henderson persuaded Armstrong to come to <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, where he became a featured and musically influential band soloist and recording artist. Hardin became Armstrong's second wife and they returned to Chicago to play together and then he began to form his own "Hot" jazz bands. After years of touring, he settled in <a href="/wiki/Queens" title="Queens">Queens</a>, and by the 1950s, he was a national musical icon, assisted in part, by his appearances on radio and in film and television, in addition to his concerts.
</p><p>With his instantly recognizable rich, gravelly voice, Armstrong was also an influential singer and skillful improviser, bending the lyrics and melody of a song. He was also skilled at <a href="/wiki/Scat_singing" title="Scat singing">scat singing</a>. Armstrong is renowned for his charismatic stage presence and voice as well as his trumpet playing. By the end of Armstrong's life, his influence had spread to popular music in general. Armstrong was one of the first popular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African-American" title="African-American">African-American</a> entertainers to "cross over" to wide popularity with white (and international) audiences. He rarely publicly politicized his race, to the dismay of fellow African Americans, but took a well-publicized stand for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Desegregation" title="Desegregation">desegregation</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Little_Rock_Nine" title="Little Rock Nine">Little Rock crisis</a>. He was able to access the upper echelons of American society at a time when this was difficult for black men.
</p><p>Armstrong appeared in films such as <i><a href="/wiki/High_Society_(1956_film)" title="High Society (1956 film)">High Society</a></i> (1956) alongside <a href="/wiki/Bing_Crosby" title="Bing Crosby">Bing Crosby</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grace_Kelly" title="Grace Kelly">Grace Kelly</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Frank_Sinatra" title="Frank Sinatra">Frank Sinatra</a>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Hello,_Dolly!_(film)" title="Hello, Dolly! (film)">Hello, Dolly!</a></i> (1969) starring <a href="/wiki/Barbra_Streisand" title="Barbra Streisand">Barbra Streisand</a>. He received many accolades including three <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grammy_Award" title="Grammy Award">Grammy Award</a> nominations and a win for his vocal performance of <i><a href="/wiki/Hello,_Dolly!_(song)" title="Hello, Dolly! (song)">Hello, Dolly!</a></i> in 1964.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> In 2017, he was posthumously inducted into the Rhythm &amp; Blues Hall of Fame.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Adi_Holzer_Werksverzeichnis_899_Satchmo_(Louis_Armstrong).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3547" data-file-width="2658" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Adi_Holzer_Werksverzeichnis_899_Satchmo_%28Louis_Armstrong%29.jpg/170px-Adi_Holzer_Werksverzeichnis_899_Satchmo_%28Louis_Armstrong%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Adi_Holzer_Werksverzeichnis_899_Satchmo_%28Louis_Armstrong%29.jpg/255px-Adi_Holzer_Werksverzeichnis_899_Satchmo_%28Louis_Armstrong%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Adi_Holzer_Werksverzeichnis_899_Satchmo_%28Louis_Armstrong%29.jpg/340px-Adi_Holzer_Werksverzeichnis_899_Satchmo_%28Louis_Armstrong%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Adi_Holzer_Werksverzeichnis_899_Satchmo_(Louis_Armstrong).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Louis Armstrong</i> (2002), hand-colored <a href="/wiki/Etching" title="Etching">etching</a> by <a class="extiw" href="https://de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adi_Holzer" title="de:Adi Holzer">Adi Holzer</a></div></div></div>
<p>Armstrong was born in <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a> on August 4, 1901.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-birthdate_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-birthdate-11">[a]</a></sup> His parents were Mary Albert and William Armstrong. Mary Albert was from <a href="/wiki/Boutte,_Louisiana" title="Boutte, Louisiana">Boutte, Louisiana</a>, and gave birth at home when she was about sixteen. William Armstrong abandoned the family shortly after.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-giddins22_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-giddins22-12">[11]</a></sup> About two years later, they had a daughter, Beatrice "Mama Lucy" Armstrong, who was raised by Albert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Louis Armstrong was raised by his grandmother until the age of five when he was returned to his mother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-giddins22_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-giddins22-12">[11]</a></sup> He spent his youth in poverty in a rough neighborhood known as The Battlefield,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> on the southern section of <a href="/wiki/Rampart_Street" title="Rampart Street">Rampart Street</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> At six he attended the Fisk School for Boys,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bergreen_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bergreen-16">[15]</a></sup> a school that accepted black children in the racially segregated system of New Orleans.
</p><p>At the age of 6, Armstrong lived with his mother and sister and worked for the Karnoffskys,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> a family of <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_Jews" title="Lithuanian Jews">Lithuanian Jews</a>, at <a href="/wiki/Karnofsky_Tailor_Shop%E2%80%93House" title="Karnofsky Tailor Shop–House">their home</a>. He would help their two sons, Morris and Alex, collect "rags and bones" and deliver coal. In 1969, while recovering from heart and kidney problems at Beth Israel Hospital in New York City, Armstrong wrote "Louis Armstrong + the Jewish Family in New Orleans, LA., the year of 1907" a memoir describing his time working for the Karnofsky family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-in-his-own-words-karnofskys_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-in-his-own-words-karnofskys-18">[17]</a></sup> Armstrong writes about singing "Russian Lullaby" with the Karnofsky family when their baby son David was put to bed and credits the family with teaching him to sing "from the heart."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-in-his-own-words-karnofskys_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-in-his-own-words-karnofskys-18">[17]</a></sup> Curiously, Armstrong quotes lyrics for it that appear to be the same as the "Russian Lullaby," copyrighted by Irving Berlin in 1927, about twenty years after Armstrong remembered singing it as a child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup> Gary Zucker, Armstrong's doctor at Beth Israel hospital in 1969, shared Berlin's song lyrics with him, and Armstrong quoted them in the memoir.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-in-his-own-words-karnofskys_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-in-his-own-words-karnofskys-18">[17]</a></sup> This inaccuracy may simply be because he wrote the memoir over 60 years after the events described.
</p><p>Regardless, the Karnoffskys treated Armstrong extremely well. Knowing he lived without a father, they fed and nurtured him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Commentary_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Commentary-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup> In his memoir <i>Louis Armstrong + the Jewish Family in New Orleans, La., the Year of 1907</i>, he described his discovery that this family was also <a href="/wiki/Racism_in_the_United_States" title="Racism in the United States">subject to discrimination</a> by "other white folks" who felt that they were better than Jews: "I was only seven years old but I could easily see the ungodly treatment that the white folks were handing the poor Jewish family whom I worked for."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-InHisOwnWords_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-InHisOwnWords-22">[21]</a></sup> He wrote about what he learned from them: "how to live—real life and determination."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Commentary_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Commentary-20">[19]</a></sup> His first musical performance may have been at the side of the Karnoffskys' junk wagon. To distinguish them from other hawkers, he tried playing a tin horn to attract customers. Morris Karnoffsky gave Armstrong an advance toward the purchase of a cornet from a pawn shop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup>
Armstrong wore a <a href="/wiki/Star_of_David" title="Star of David">Star of David</a> until the end of his life in memory of this family who had raised him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>When Armstrong was eleven, he dropped out of school.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bergreen_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bergreen-16">[15]</a></sup> His mother moved into a one-room house on Perdido Street with Armstrong, Lucy, and her common-law husband, Tom Lee, next door to her brother Ike and his two sons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup> Armstrong joined a quartet of boys who sang in the streets for money. He also got into trouble. Cornetist <a href="/wiki/Bunk_Johnson" title="Bunk Johnson">Bunk Johnson</a> said he taught the eleven-year-old to play by ear at Dago Tony's honky tonk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup> (In his later years Armstrong credited King Oliver.) He said about his youth, "Every time I close my eyes blowing that trumpet of mine—I look right in the heart of good old New Orleans ... It has given me something to live for."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[26]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LouisArmstrong_PeterDavis1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="264" data-file-width="377" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d0/LouisArmstrong_PeterDavis1.jpg/220px-LouisArmstrong_PeterDavis1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/d/d0/LouisArmstrong_PeterDavis1.jpg/330px-LouisArmstrong_PeterDavis1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/d/d0/LouisArmstrong_PeterDavis1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LouisArmstrong_PeterDavis1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Armstrong with his first trumpet instructor, Peter Davis, in 1965</div></div></div>
<p>Borrowing his stepfather's gun without permission, he fired a blank into the air and was arrested on December 31, 1912. He spent the night at New Orleans Juvenile Court, then was sentenced the next day to detention at the Colored Waif's Home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup> Life at the home was spartan. Mattresses were absent; meals were often little more than bread and molasses. Captain Joseph Jones ran the home like a military camp and used corporal punishment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Armstrong developed his cornet skills by playing in the band. Peter Davis, who frequently appeared at the home at the request of Captain Jones,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup> became Armstrong's first teacher and chose him as bandleader. With this band, the thirteen-year-old Armstrong attracted the attention of <a href="/wiki/Kid_Ory" title="Kid Ory">Kid Ory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>On June 14, 1914, Armstrong was released into the custody of his father and his new stepmother, Gertrude. He lived in this household with two stepbrothers for several months. After Gertrude gave birth to a daughter, Armstrong's father never welcomed him, so he returned to his mother, Mary Albert. In her small home, he had to share a bed with his mother and sister.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[31]</a></sup> His mother still lived in The Battlefield, leaving him open to old temptations, but he sought work as a musician. He found a job at a dance hall owned by Henry Ponce, who had connections to organized crime. He met the six-foot tall drummer <a href="/wiki/Black_Benny" title="Black Benny">Black Benny</a>, who became his guide and bodyguard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> Around the age of fifteen, he pimped for a prostitute named Nootsy, but that relationship failed after she stabbed Armstrong in the shoulder and his mother choked her nearly to death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>He briefly studied shipping management at the local community college, but was forced to quit after being unable to afford the fees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bergreen_1997,_p.44_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bergreen_1997,_p.44-35">[34]</a></sup> While selling coal in <a href="/wiki/Storyville,_New_Orleans" title="Storyville, New Orleans">Storyville</a>, he heard <a href="/wiki/Spasm_band" title="Spasm band">spasm bands</a>, groups that played music out of household objects. He heard the early sounds of jazz from bands that played in brothels and dance halls such as Pete Lala's, where <a href="/wiki/King_Oliver" title="King Oliver">King Oliver</a> performed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bergreen_1997_pp.45_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bergreen_1997_pp.45-36">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career">Career</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Riverboat_education">Riverboat education</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fate_Marable%27s_New_Orleans_Band_on_the_S._S._Sidney.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="658" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Fate_Marable%27s_New_Orleans_Band_on_the_S._S._Sidney.jpg/220px-Fate_Marable%27s_New_Orleans_Band_on_the_S._S._Sidney.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Fate_Marable%27s_New_Orleans_Band_on_the_S._S._Sidney.jpg/330px-Fate_Marable%27s_New_Orleans_Band_on_the_S._S._Sidney.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Fate_Marable%27s_New_Orleans_Band_on_the_S._S._Sidney.jpg/440px-Fate_Marable%27s_New_Orleans_Band_on_the_S._S._Sidney.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fate_Marable%27s_New_Orleans_Band_on_the_S._S._Sidney.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Armstrong was a member of Fate Marable's New Orleans Band in 1918, here on board the S.S. <i>Sidney</i></div></div></div>
<p>Early in his career, Armstrong played in brass bands and <a href="/wiki/Riverboat" title="Riverboat">riverboats</a> in New Orleans, first on an excursion boat in September 1918. He traveled with the band of <a href="/wiki/Fate_Marable" title="Fate Marable">Fate Marable</a>, which toured on the steamboat <i>Sidney</i> with the <a href="/wiki/Streckfus_Steamers" title="Streckfus Steamers">Streckfus Steamers</a> line up and down the Mississippi River.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[36]</a></sup> Marable was proud of his musical knowledge, and he insisted that Armstrong and other musicians in his band learn <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sight_reading" title="Sight reading">sight reading</a>. Armstrong described his time with Marable as "going to the University", since it gave him a wider experience working with written <a href="/wiki/Arrangement" title="Arrangement">arrangements</a>. In 1919, Armstrong's mentor, King Oliver decided to go north and resigned his position in Kid Ory's band; Armstrong replaced him. He also became second trumpet for the <a href="/wiki/Tuxedo_Brass_Band" title="Tuxedo Brass Band">Tuxedo Brass Band</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout his riverboat experience, Armstrong's musicianship began to mature and expand. At twenty, he could read music. He became one of the first jazz musicians to be featured on extended trumpet solos, injecting his own personality and style. He also started singing in his performances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chicago_and_recording_for_Gennett">Chicago and recording for Gennett</span></h3>
<p>In 1922, Armstrong moved to Chicago at the invitation of King Oliver,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collier_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collier-40">[39]</a></sup> although Armstrong would return to New Orleans periodically for the rest of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup> Playing second cornet to Oliver in Oliver's Creole Jazz Band in the black-only Lincoln Gardens in Chicago's black neighborhood, he could make enough money to quit his day jobs. Although race relations were poor, Chicago was booming. The city had jobs for blacks making good wages at factories with some left over for entertainment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collier_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Collier-40">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 86">: 86 </span></sup>
</p><p>Oliver's band was among the most influential jazz bands in Chicago in the early 1920s. Armstrong lived luxuriously in his own apartment with his first private bath. Excited as he was to be in Chicago, he began his career-long pastime of writing letters to friends in New Orleans. Armstrong could blow two hundred <a href="/wiki/C_(musical_note)#Other_octaves" title="C (musical note)">high Cs</a> in a row. As his reputation grew, he was challenged to <a href="/wiki/Cutting_contest" title="Cutting contest">cutting contests</a> by other musicians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[41]</a></sup>
</p><p>His first studio recordings were with Oliver for <a href="/wiki/Gennett_Records" title="Gennett Records">Gennett Records</a> on April 5–6, 1923. They endured several hours on the train to remote <a href="/wiki/Richmond,_Indiana" title="Richmond, Indiana">Richmond, Indiana</a>, and the band was paid little. The quality of the performances was affected by lack of rehearsal, crude recording equipment, bad acoustics, and a cramped studio. These early recordings were true <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gramophone_record#78_rpm_disc_developments" title="Gramophone record">acoustic</a>, the band playing directly into a large funnel connected directly to the needle making the groove in the master recording. (Electrical recording was not invented until 1926 and Gennett installed it later.) Because Armstrong's playing was so loud, when he played next to Oliver, Oliver couldn't be heard on the recording. Armstrong had to stand fifteen feet away from Oliver, in a far corner of the room.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Lil_Hardin_Armstrong" title="Lil Hardin Armstrong">Lil Hardin</a>, who Armstrong would marry in 1924, urged Armstrong to seek more prominent billing and develop his style apart from the influence of Oliver. At her suggestion, Armstrong began to play classical music in church concerts to broaden his skills; and he began to dress more in more stylish attire to offset his girth. Her influence eventually undermined Armstrong's relationship with his mentor, especially concerning his salary and additional money that Oliver held back from Armstrong and other band members.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> Armstrong's mother, May Ann Albert, came to visit him in Chicago during the summer of 1923 after being told that Armstrong was "out of work, out of money, hungry, and sick"; Hardin located and decorated an apartment for her to live in while she stayed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_Fletcher_Henderson_Orchestra">In the Fletcher Henderson Orchestra</span></h3>
<p>Armstrong and Oliver parted amicably in 1924. Shortly afterward, Armstrong received an invitation to go to New York City to play with the <a href="/wiki/Fletcher_Henderson" title="Fletcher Henderson">Fletcher Henderson</a> Orchestra, the top African-American band of the time. He switched to the trumpet to blend in better with the other musicians in his section. His influence on Henderson's tenor sax soloist, <a href="/wiki/Coleman_Hawkins" title="Coleman Hawkins">Coleman Hawkins</a>, can be judged by listening to the records made by the band during this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Armstrong adapted to the tightly controlled style of Henderson, playing trumpet and experimenting with the trombone. The other members were affected by Armstrong's emotional style. His act included singing and telling tales of New Orleans characters, especially preachers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[47]</a></sup> The Henderson Orchestra played in prominent venues for white patrons only, including the <a href="/wiki/Roseland_Ballroom" title="Roseland Ballroom">Roseland Ballroom</a>, with arrangements by <a href="/wiki/Don_Redman" title="Don Redman">Don Redman</a>. Duke Ellington's orchestra went to Roseland to catch Armstrong's performances.
</p><p>During this time, Armstrong recorded with <a href="/wiki/Clarence_Williams_(musician)" title="Clarence Williams (musician)">Clarence Williams</a> (a friend from New Orleans), the Williams Blue Five, <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Bechet" title="Sidney Bechet">Sidney Bechet</a>, and blues singers <a href="/wiki/Alberta_Hunter" title="Alberta Hunter">Alberta Hunter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ma_Rainey" title="Ma Rainey">Ma Rainey</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bessie_Smith" title="Bessie Smith">Bessie Smith</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Hot_Five">The Hot Five</span></h3>
<p>In 1925, Armstrong returned to Chicago largely at the insistence of Lil, who wanted to expand his career and his income. In publicity, much to his chagrin, she billed him as "the World's Greatest Trumpet Player". For a time he was a member of the Lil Hardin Armstrong Band and working for his wife.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> He formed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_Armstrong_and_his_Hot_Five" title="Louis Armstrong and his Hot Five">Louis Armstrong and his Hot Five</a> and recorded the hits "<a href="/wiki/Potato_Head_Blues" title="Potato Head Blues">Potato Head Blues</a>" and "<a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Muggles_(recording)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Muggles (recording) (page does not exist)">Muggles</a>". The word "muggles" was a <a href="/wiki/List_of_slang_names_for_cannabis" title="List of slang names for cannabis">slang term for marijuana</a>, something he used often during his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collier_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-Collier-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Heebie_Jeebies.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="308" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Heebie_Jeebies.jpg/170px-Heebie_Jeebies.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Heebie_Jeebies.jpg/255px-Heebie_Jeebies.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/da/Heebie_Jeebies.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Heebie_Jeebies.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"<a href="/wiki/Heebie_Jeebies_(composition)" title="Heebie Jeebies (composition)">Heebie Jeebies</a>" by Louis Armstrong and his Hot Five</div></div></div>
<p>The Hot Five included <a href="/wiki/Kid_Ory" title="Kid Ory">Kid Ory</a> (trombone), <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Dodds" title="Johnny Dodds">Johnny Dodds</a> (clarinet), <a href="/wiki/Johnny_St._Cyr" title="Johnny St. Cyr">Johnny St. Cyr</a> (banjo), Lil Armstrong on piano, and usually no drummer. Over a twelve-month period starting in November 1925, this quintet produced twenty-four records.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harker_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-harker-52">[51]</a></sup> Armstrong's band leading style was easygoing, as St. Cyr noted, "One felt so relaxed working with him, and he was very broad-minded ... always did his best to feature each individual."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup> Among the most notable of the Hot Five and Seven records were "Cornet Chop Suey", "Struttin' With Some Barbecue", "Hotter Than that" and "Potato Head Blues", all featuring highly creative solos by Armstrong. According to Thomas Brothers, recordings, such as "Struttin' with Some Barbeque", were so superb, "planned with density and variety, bluesyness, and showiness," that the arrangements were probably showcased at the Sunset Café.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> His recordings soon after with pianist <a href="/wiki/Earl_Hines" title="Earl Hines">Earl "Fatha" Hines</a>, most famously their 1928 "<a href="/wiki/Weather_Bird" title="Weather Bird">Weather Bird</a>" duet and Armstrong's trumpet introduction to and solo in "West End Blues", remain some of the most famous and influential improvisations in jazz history. Young trumpet players across the country bought these recordings and memorized his solos.
</p><p>Armstrong was now free to develop his personal style as he wished, which included a heavy dose of effervescent jive, such as "Whip That Thing, Miss Lil" and "Mr. Johnny Dodds, Aw, Do That Clarinet, Boy!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Armstrong also played with <a href="/wiki/Erskine_Tate" title="Erskine Tate">Erskine Tate</a>'s Little Symphony, which played mostly at the Vendome Theatre. They furnished music for silent movies and live shows, including jazz versions of classical music, such as "Madame Butterfly", which gave Armstrong experience with longer forms of music and with hosting before a large audience. He began to scat sing (improvise vocal jazz using nonsensical words) and was among the first to record it, on the Hot Five recording "<a href="/wiki/Heebie_Jeebies_(composition)" title="Heebie Jeebies (composition)">Heebie Jeebies</a>" in 1926. The recording was so popular that the group became the most famous jazz band in the United States, even though they had not performed live to any great extent. Young musicians across the country, black or white, were turned on by Armstrong's new type of jazz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>After separating from Lil, Armstrong started to play at the <a href="/wiki/Sunset_Cafe" title="Sunset Cafe">Sunset Café</a> for <a href="/wiki/Al_Capone" title="Al Capone">Al Capone</a>'s associate <a href="/wiki/Joe_Glaser" title="Joe Glaser">Joe Glaser</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Carroll_Dickerson" title="Carroll Dickerson">Carroll Dickerson</a> Orchestra, with <a href="/wiki/Earl_Hines" title="Earl Hines">Earl Hines</a> on piano, which was renamed Louis Armstrong and his Stompers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> though Hines was the music director and Glaser managed the orchestra. Hines and Armstrong became fast friends and successful collaborators. It was at the <a href="/wiki/Sunset_Cafe" title="Sunset Cafe">Sunset Café</a> that Armstrong accompanied singer <a href="/wiki/Adelaide_Hall" title="Adelaide Hall">Adelaide Hall</a>. It was during Hall's tenure at the venue that she experimented, developed and expanded her use and art of scat singing with Armstrong's guidance and encouragement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the first half of 1927, Armstrong assembled his Hot Seven group, which added drummer Al "Baby" Dodds and tuba player, Pete Briggs, while preserving most of his original Hot Five lineup. John Thomas replaced Kid Ory on trombone. Later that year he organized a series of new Hot Five sessions which resulted in nine more records. In the last half of 1928, he started recording with a new group: Zutty Singleton (drums), Earl Hines (piano), Jimmy Strong (clarinet), Fred Robinson (trombone), and Mancy Carr (banjo).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Harlem_Renaissance">The Harlem Renaissance</span></h3>
<p>Armstrong made a huge impact during the 1920s <a href="/wiki/Harlem_Renaissance" title="Harlem Renaissance">Harlem Renaissance</a> within the Jazz world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup> His music touched many, including a well-known writer during that time named <a href="/wiki/Langston_Hughes" title="Langston Hughes">Langston Hughes</a>. Hughes admired Armstrong and acknowledged him as one of the most recognized musicians during the era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> Hughes created many books that upheld the central idea of jazz and gave recognition to Armstrong as one of the most important persons to be part of the Harlem Renaissance's newfound love of African-American culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup> The sound of jazz, along with many other musicians such as Armstrong, helped shape Hughes as a writer. Just like the musicians, Hughes wrote his words with jazz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Armstrong changed jazz during the Harlem Renaissance. Being known as "The World's Greatest Trumpet Player" during this time,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup> Armstrong continued his legacy and continued a focus on his own vocal career. The popularity he gained brought together many black and white audiences to watch him perform.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emerging_as_a_vocalist">Emerging as a vocalist</span></h3>
<p>Armstrong returned to New York in 1929, where he played in the pit orchestra for the musical <i>Hot Chocolates</i>, an all-black revue written by <a href="/wiki/Andy_Razaf" title="Andy Razaf">Andy Razaf</a> and pianist <a href="/wiki/Fats_Waller" title="Fats Waller">Fats Waller</a>. He also made a cameo appearance as a vocalist, regularly stealing the show with his rendition of "<a href="/wiki/Ain%27t_Misbehavin%27_(song)" title="Ain't Misbehavin' (song)">Ain't Misbehavin'</a>". His version of the song became his biggest selling record to date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Armstrong started to work at <a href="/wiki/Connie%27s_Inn" title="Connie's Inn">Connie's Inn</a> in Harlem, chief rival to the <a href="/wiki/Cotton_Club" title="Cotton Club">Cotton Club</a>, a venue for elaborately staged floor shows,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-morgenstern_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-morgenstern-67">[66]</a></sup> and a front for gangster <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Schultz" title="Dutch Schultz">Dutch Schultz</a>. Armstrong also had considerable success with vocal recordings, including versions of songs composed by his old friend <a href="/wiki/Hoagy_Carmichael" title="Hoagy Carmichael">Hoagy Carmichael</a>. His 1930s recordings took full advantage of the new RCA <a href="/wiki/Ribbon_microphone" title="Ribbon microphone">ribbon microphone</a>, introduced in 1931, which imparted a characteristic warmth to vocals and immediately became an intrinsic part of the '<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crooning" title="Crooning">crooning</a>' sound of artists like <a href="/wiki/Bing_Crosby" title="Bing Crosby">Bing Crosby</a>. Armstrong's interpretation of Carmichael's "<a href="/wiki/Stardust_(1927_song)" title="Stardust (1927 song)">Stardust</a>" became one of the most successful versions of this song ever recorded, showcasing Armstrong's unique vocal sound and style and his innovative approach to singing songs that had already become standards.
</p><p>Armstrong's radical re-working of <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Arodin" title="Sidney Arodin">Sidney Arodin</a> and Carmichael's "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lazy_River" title="Lazy River">Lazy River</a>" (recorded in 1931) encapsulated many features of his groundbreaking approach to melody and phrasing. The song begins with a brief trumpet solo, then the main melody is introduced by sobbing horns, memorably punctuated by Armstrong's growling interjections at the end of each bar: "Yeah! ..."Uh-huh"..."Sure"..."Way down, way down." In the first verse, he ignores the notated melody entirely and sings as if playing a trumpet solo, pitching most of the first line on a single note and using strongly syncopated phrasing. In the second stanza he breaks into an almost fully improvised melody, which then evolves into a classic passage of Armstrong "<a href="/wiki/Scat_singing" title="Scat singing">scat singing</a>".
</p><p>As with his trumpet playing, Armstrong's vocal innovations served as a foundation stone for the art of jazz vocal interpretation. The uniquely gravelly coloration of his voice became a musical archetype that was much imitated and endlessly impersonated. His scat singing style was enriched by his matchless experience as a trumpet soloist. His resonant, velvety lower-register tone and bubbling cadences on sides such as "Lazy River" exerted a huge influence on younger white singers such as Bing Crosby.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Working_during_hard_times">Working during hard times</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maud_Cuney_Hare-154-Louis_Armstrong.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1577" data-file-width="1030" decoding="async" height="337" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Maud_Cuney_Hare-154-Louis_Armstrong.jpg/220px-Maud_Cuney_Hare-154-Louis_Armstrong.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Maud_Cuney_Hare-154-Louis_Armstrong.jpg/330px-Maud_Cuney_Hare-154-Louis_Armstrong.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Maud_Cuney_Hare-154-Louis_Armstrong.jpg/440px-Maud_Cuney_Hare-154-Louis_Armstrong.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maud_Cuney_Hare-154-Louis_Armstrong.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Photo of Armstrong in 1936</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> of the early 1930s was especially hard on the jazz scene. The Cotton Club closed in 1936 after a long downward spiral, and many musicians stopped playing altogether as club dates evaporated. Bix Beiderbecke died and Fletcher Henderson's band broke up. King Oliver made a few records but otherwise struggled. <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Bechet" title="Sidney Bechet">Sidney Bechet</a> became a tailor, later moving to Paris and Kid Ory returned to New Orleans and raised chickens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Armstrong moved to Los Angeles in 1930 to seek new opportunities. He played at the New Cotton Club in Los Angeles with <a href="/wiki/Lionel_Hampton" title="Lionel Hampton">Lionel Hampton</a> on drums. The band drew the Hollywood crowd, which could still afford a lavish night life, while radio broadcasts from the club connected with younger audiences at home. Bing Crosby and many other celebrities were regulars at the club. In 1931, Armstrong appeared in his first movie, <i>Ex-Flame</i> and was also convicted of marijuana possession but received a suspended sentence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup> He returned to Chicago in late 1931 and played in bands more in the <a href="/wiki/Guy_Lombardo" title="Guy Lombardo">Guy Lombardo</a> vein and he recorded more standards. When the mob insisted that he get out of town,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ArmstrongInThe30s_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-ArmstrongInThe30s-70">[69]</a></sup> Armstrong visited New Orleans, had a hero's welcome, and saw old friends. He sponsored a local baseball team known as Armstrong's Secret Nine and had a cigar named after him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup> But soon he was on the road again. After a tour across the country shadowed by the mob, he fled to Europe.
</p><p>After returning to the United States, he undertook several exhausting tours. His agent Johnny Collins's erratic behavior and his own spending ways left Armstrong short of cash. Breach of contract violations plagued him. He hired Joe Glaser as his new manager, a tough mob-connected wheeler-dealer, who began to straighten out his legal mess, his mob troubles, and his debts. Armstrong also began to experience problems with his fingers and lips, which were aggravated by his unorthodox playing style. As a result, he branched out, developing his vocal style and making his first theatrical appearances. He appeared in movies again, including Crosby's 1936 hit <i><a href="/wiki/Pennies_from_Heaven_(1936_film)" title="Pennies from Heaven (1936 film)">Pennies from Heaven</a></i>. In 1937, Armstrong substituted for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rudy_Vallee" title="Rudy Vallee">Rudy Vallee</a> on the CBS radio network and became the first African American to host a sponsored, national broadcast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reviving_his_career_with_the_All_Stars">Reviving his career with the All Stars</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Armstrong2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3774" data-file-width="2933" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Louis_Armstrong2.jpg/170px-Louis_Armstrong2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Louis_Armstrong2.jpg/255px-Louis_Armstrong2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Louis_Armstrong2.jpg/340px-Louis_Armstrong2.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Armstrong2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Armstrong in 1953</div></div></div>
<p>After spending many years on the road, Armstrong settled permanently in Queens, New York in 1943 in contentment with his fourth wife, Lucille. Although subject to the vicissitudes of <a href="/wiki/Tin_Pan_Alley" title="Tin Pan Alley">Tin Pan Alley</a> and the gangster-ridden music business, as well as anti-black prejudice, he continued to develop his playing.
</p><p>Bookings for big bands tapered off during the 1940s due to changes in public tastes: ballrooms closed, and there was competition from other types of music, especially pop vocals, becoming more popular than big band music. It became impossible under such circumstances to finance a 16-piece touring band.
</p><p>A widespread revival of interest in the 1940s in the traditional jazz of the 1920s made it possible for Armstrong to consider a return to the small-group musical style of his youth. Armstrong was featured as a guest artist with Lionel Hampton's band at the famed second Cavalcade of Jazz concert held at <a href="/wiki/Wrigley_Field_(Los_Angeles)" title="Wrigley Field (Los Angeles)">Wrigley Field</a> in Los Angeles which was produced by <a href="/wiki/Leon_Hefflin,_Sr." title="Leon Hefflin, Sr.">Leon Hefflin Sr.</a> on October 12, 1946.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup> He also led a highly successful small-group jazz concert at New York Town Hall on May 17, 1947, featuring Armstrong with trombonist/singer <a href="/wiki/Jack_Teagarden" title="Jack Teagarden">Jack Teagarden</a>. During the concert, Armstrong and Teagarden performed a duet on Hoagy Carmichael's "<a href="/wiki/Rockin%27_Chair_(1929_song)" title="Rockin' Chair (1929 song)">Rockin' Chair</a>" they then recorded for <a href="/wiki/Okeh_Records" title="Okeh Records">Okeh Records</a>.
</p><p>Armstrong's manager, Joe Glaser dissolved the Armstrong big band on August 13, 1947, and established a six-piece traditional jazz group featuring Armstrong with (initially) Teagarden, <a href="/wiki/Earl_Hines" title="Earl Hines">Earl Hines</a> and other top swing and Dixieland musicians, most of whom were previously leaders of big bands. The new group was announced at the opening of <a href="/wiki/Billy_Berg%27s" title="Billy Berg's">Billy Berg's</a> Supper Club.
</p><p>This group was called Louis Armstrong and His All Stars and included at various times <a href="/wiki/Earl_Hines" title="Earl Hines">Earl "Fatha" Hines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barney_Bigard" title="Barney Bigard">Barney Bigard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Edmond_Hall" title="Edmond Hall">Edmond Hall</a>, Jack Teagarden, <a href="/wiki/Trummy_Young" title="Trummy Young">Trummy Young</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arvell_Shaw" title="Arvell Shaw">Arvell Shaw</a>, <a href="/wiki/Billy_Kyle" title="Billy Kyle">Billy Kyle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marty_Napoleon" title="Marty Napoleon">Marty Napoleon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sid_Catlett" title="Sid Catlett">Big Sid "Buddy" Catlett</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cozy_Cole" title="Cozy Cole">Cozy Cole</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tyree_Glenn" title="Tyree Glenn">Tyree Glenn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barrett_Deems" title="Barrett Deems">Barrett Deems</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mort_Herbert" title="Mort Herbert">Mort Herbert</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joe_Darensbourg" title="Joe Darensbourg">Joe Darensbourg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Shu" title="Eddie Shu">Eddie Shu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Joe_Muranyi" title="Joe Muranyi">Joe Muranyi</a> and percussionist <a href="/wiki/Danny_Barcelona" title="Danny Barcelona">Danny Barcelona</a>.
</p><p>On February 28, 1948, <a href="/wiki/Suzy_Delair" title="Suzy Delair">Suzy Delair</a> sang the French song <i><a href="/wiki/C%27est_si_bon" title="C'est si bon">C'est si bon</a></i> at the <a href="/wiki/Hotel_Negresco" title="Hotel Negresco">Hotel Negresco</a> during the first <a href="/wiki/Nice_Jazz_Festival" title="Nice Jazz Festival">Nice Jazz Festival</a>. Louis Armstrong was present and loved the song. On June 26, 1950, he recorded the American version of the song (English lyrics by <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Seelen" title="Jerry Seelen">Jerry Seelen</a>) in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> with <a href="/wiki/Sy_Oliver" title="Sy Oliver">Sy Oliver</a> and his Orchestra. When it was released, the disc was a worldwide success and the song was then performed by the greatest international singers.
</p><p>He was the first jazz musician to appear on the cover of <i>Time</i> magazine, on February 21, 1949. Louis Armstrong and his All Stars were featured at the ninth Cavalcade of Jazz concert also at <a href="/wiki/Wrigley_Field_(Los_Angeles)" title="Wrigley Field (Los Angeles)">Wrigley Field</a> in Los Angeles produced by <a href="/wiki/Leon_Hefflin,_Sr." title="Leon Hefflin, Sr.">Leon Hefflin Sr.</a> held on June 7, 1953, along with <a href="/wiki/Shorty_Rogers" title="Shorty Rogers">Shorty Rogers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roy_Brown_(blues_musician)" title="Roy Brown (blues musician)">Roy Brown</a>, Don Tosti and His Mexican Jazzmen, <a href="/wiki/Earl_Bostic" title="Earl Bostic">Earl Bostic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nat_King_Cole" title="Nat King Cole">Nat "King" Cole</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the next 30 years, Armstrong played more than 300 performances a year. During this period, Armstrong made many recordings and appeared in over thirty films.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="A_jazz_ambassador">A jazz ambassador</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Armstrong_(1955).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1712" data-file-width="2282" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Louis_Armstrong_%281955%29.jpg/220px-Louis_Armstrong_%281955%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Louis_Armstrong_%281955%29.jpg/330px-Louis_Armstrong_%281955%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Louis_Armstrong_%281955%29.jpg/440px-Louis_Armstrong_%281955%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Armstrong_(1955).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Armstrong in 1955</div></div></div>
<p>By the 1950s, Armstrong was a widely beloved American icon and cultural ambassador who commanded an international fanbase. However, a growing generation gap became apparent between him and the young jazz musicians who emerged in the postwar era such as <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Parker" title="Charlie Parker">Charlie Parker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miles_Davis" title="Miles Davis">Miles Davis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sonny_Rollins" title="Sonny Rollins">Sonny Rollins</a>. The postwar generation regarded their music as abstract art and considered Armstrong's vaudevillian style, half-musician and half-stage entertainer, outmoded and <a href="/wiki/Uncle_Tom_syndrome" title="Uncle Tom syndrome">Uncle Tomism</a>, "... he seemed a link to <a href="/wiki/Minstrel_show" title="Minstrel show">minstrelsy</a> that we were ashamed of."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Starkey2015_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Starkey2015-75">[74]</a></sup> He called bebop "Chinese music".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup> While touring Australia in 1954, he was asked if he could play bebop. "Bebop?" he husked. "I just play music. Guys who invent terms like that are walking the streets with their instruments under their arms."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reception_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-reception-77">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mack_The_Knife_Coronet.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="400" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Mack_The_Knife_Coronet.jpg/200px-Mack_The_Knife_Coronet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Mack_The_Knife_Coronet.jpg/300px-Mack_The_Knife_Coronet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f7/Mack_The_Knife_Coronet.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mack_The_Knife_Coronet.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"<a href="/wiki/Mack_the_Knife" title="Mack the Knife">Mack the Knife</a>" was released in 1956.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ficha_Consular_de_Louis_Armstrong.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5912" data-file-width="9208" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Ficha_Consular_de_Louis_Armstrong.jpg/220px-Ficha_Consular_de_Louis_Armstrong.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Ficha_Consular_de_Louis_Armstrong.jpg/330px-Ficha_Consular_de_Louis_Armstrong.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Ficha_Consular_de_Louis_Armstrong.jpg/440px-Ficha_Consular_de_Louis_Armstrong.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ficha_Consular_de_Louis_Armstrong.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Record of Armstrong's visit to Brazil, 1957.</div></div></div>
<p>In the 1960s, he toured <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kelley2012_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kelley2012-78">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Company1971_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Company1971-79">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>After finishing his contract with <a href="/wiki/Decca_Records" title="Decca Records">Decca Records</a>, he became a freelance artist and recorded for other labels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nollen2004_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nollen2004-80">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup> He continued an intense international touring schedule, but in 1959 he suffered a heart attack in Italy and had to rest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1964, after over two years without setting foot in a studio, he recorded his biggest-selling record, "<a href="/wiki/Hello,_Dolly!_(song)" title="Hello, Dolly! (song)">Hello, Dolly!</a>", a song by <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Herman" title="Jerry Herman">Jerry Herman</a>, originally sung by <a href="/wiki/Carol_Channing" title="Carol Channing">Carol Channing</a>. Armstrong's version remained on the Hot 100 for 22 weeks, longer than any other record produced that year, and went to No. 1 making him, at 62 years, 9 months and 5 days, the oldest person ever to accomplish that feat. In the process, he dislodged <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">the Beatles</a> from the No. 1 position they had occupied for 14 consecutive weeks with three different songs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hale_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hale-83">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="infobox" style="width: 210px; float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.5em 1.5em"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-above" colspan="2" style="font-size:115%">External audio</th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2" style="text-align: left"><img alt="audio icon" data-file-height="128" data-file-width="128" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Nuvola_apps_arts.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_arts.svg.png 2x" width="16"/> <a class="external text" href="https://soundcloud.com/studsterkel-radio-archive/louis-daniel-armstrong-talks-with-studs-terkel-on-wfmt-1962624" rel="nofollow">Louis Daniel Armstrong talks with Studs Terkel on WFMT; 1962/6/24</a>, 33:43, <a href="/wiki/Studs_Terkel_Radio_Archive" title="Studs Terkel Radio Archive">Studs Terkel Radio Archive</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-studs_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-studs-84">[83]</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Armstrong kept touring well into his 60s, even visiting part of the communist bloc in 1965. He also toured Africa, Europe, and Asia under the <a href="/wiki/Jazz_ambassadors" title="Jazz ambassadors">sponsorship of the US State Department</a> with great success, earning the nickname "Ambassador Satch" and inspiring Dave Brubeck to compose his jazz musical <i><a href="/wiki/The_Real_Ambassadors" title="The Real Ambassadors">The Real Ambassadors</a></i>. By 1968, he was approaching 70 and his health began to give out. He suffered heart and kidney ailments that forced him to stop touring. He did not perform publicly at all in 1969 and spent most of the year recuperating at home. Meanwhile, his longtime manager <a href="/wiki/Joe_Glaser" title="Joe Glaser">Joe Glaser</a> died. By the summer of 1970, his doctors pronounced him fit enough to resume live performances. He embarked on another world tour, but a heart attack forced him to take a break for two months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Von_Eschen_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Von_Eschen-85">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>Armstrong made his last recorded trumpet performances on his 1968 album <i><a href="/wiki/Disney_Songs_the_Satchmo_Way" title="Disney Songs the Satchmo Way">Disney Songs the Satchmo Way</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whitaker2011_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whitaker2011-86">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_life">Personal life</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pronunciation_of_name">Pronunciation of name</span></h3>
<p>The Louis Armstrong House Museum website states:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Judging from home recorded tapes now in our Museum Collections, Louis pronounced his own name as "Lewis". On his 1964 record "Hello, Dolly", he sings, "This is Lewis, Dolly" but in 1933 he made a record called "Laughin' Louie". Many broadcast announcers, fans, and acquaintances called him "Louie" and in a videotaped interview from 1983 Lucille Armstrong calls her late husband "Louie" as well. Musicians and close friends usually called him "Pops".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>In a memoir written for <a href="/wiki/Robert_Goffin" title="Robert Goffin">Robert Goffin</a> between 1943 and 1944, Armstrong states, "All white folks call me Louie," perhaps suggesting that he himself did not or, on the other hand, that no whites addressed him by one of his nicknames such as Pops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup> That said, Armstrong was registered as "Lewie" for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_Census,_1920" title="United States Census, 1920">1920 U.S. Census</a>. On various live records he's called "Louie" on stage, such as on the 1952 "Can Anyone Explain?" from the live album <i>In Scandinavia vol.1</i>. The same applies to his 1952 studio recording of the song "Chloe", where the choir in the background sings "Louie ... Louie", with Armstrong responding "What was that? Somebody called my name?" "Lewie" is the French pronunciation of "Louis" and is commonly used in Louisiana.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family">Family</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lucille_Wilson_and_Louis_Armstrong.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2530" data-file-width="3543" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Lucille_Wilson_and_Louis_Armstrong.jpg/220px-Lucille_Wilson_and_Louis_Armstrong.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Lucille_Wilson_and_Louis_Armstrong.jpg/330px-Lucille_Wilson_and_Louis_Armstrong.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Lucille_Wilson_and_Louis_Armstrong.jpg/440px-Lucille_Wilson_and_Louis_Armstrong.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lucille_Wilson_and_Louis_Armstrong.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Armstrong with Lucille Wilson (c. 1960s)</div></div></div>
<p>Armstrong was performing at the Brick House in <a href="/wiki/Gretna,_Louisiana" title="Gretna, Louisiana">Gretna, Louisiana</a>, when he met Daisy Parker, a local prostitute. He started the affair as a client. He returned to Gretna on several occasions to visit her. He found the courage to look for her home to see her away from work. It was on this occasion he found out she had a common-law husband. Not long after this fiasco, Parker traveled to Armstrong's home on Perdido Street.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bergreen134_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-bergreen134-89">[88]</a></sup> They checked into Kid Green's hotel that evening. On the next day, March 19, 1919, Armstrong and Parker married at City Hall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bergreen134_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-bergreen134-89">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collier2_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collier2-90">[89]</a></sup> They adopted a three-year-old boy, Clarence, whose mother, Armstrong's cousin Flora, had died soon after giving birth. Clarence Armstrong was mentally disabled as the result of a head injury at an early age, and Armstrong spent the rest of his life taking care of him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup> His marriage to Parker ended when they separated in 1923.
</p><p>On February 4, 1924, he married <a href="/wiki/Lil_Hardin_Armstrong" title="Lil Hardin Armstrong">Lil Hardin Armstrong</a>, King Oliver's pianist. She had divorced her first husband a few years earlier. His second wife helped him develop his career, but they separated in 1931 and divorced in 1938. Armstrong then married Alpha Smith.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup> His relationship with Alpha began while he was playing at the Vendome during the 1920s and continued long after.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup> His marriage to her lasted four years; they divorced in 1942. Louis then married Lucille Wilson, a singer at the <a href="/wiki/Cotton_Club" title="Cotton Club">Cotton Club</a> in New York, in October 1942; they remained married until his death in 1971.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>Armstrong's marriages never produced any offspring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup> However, in December 2012, 57-year-old Sharon Preston-Folta claimed to be his daughter from a 1950s affair between Armstrong and Lucille "Sweets" Preston, a dancer at the Cotton Club.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup> In a 1955 letter to his manager, Joe Glaser, Armstrong affirmed his belief that Preston's newborn baby was his daughter, and ordered Glaser to pay a monthly allowance of $400 ($5,058 in 2021 dollars<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inflation-US_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-97">[96]</a></sup>) to mother and child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collier3_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collier3-98">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personality">Personality</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:10-08-1952_11029_Louis_Armstrong_(4489142487).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="790" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/10-08-1952_11029_Louis_Armstrong_%284489142487%29.jpg/220px-10-08-1952_11029_Louis_Armstrong_%284489142487%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/10-08-1952_11029_Louis_Armstrong_%284489142487%29.jpg/330px-10-08-1952_11029_Louis_Armstrong_%284489142487%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/10-08-1952_11029_Louis_Armstrong_%284489142487%29.jpg/440px-10-08-1952_11029_Louis_Armstrong_%284489142487%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:10-08-1952_11029_Louis_Armstrong_(4489142487).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Armstrong in Belgium in 1952</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Armstrong_visit_to_Israel,_1959_(997009327082505171).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1547" data-file-width="1571" decoding="async" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Louis_Armstrong_visit_to_Israel%2C_1959_%28997009327082505171%29.jpg/220px-Louis_Armstrong_visit_to_Israel%2C_1959_%28997009327082505171%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Louis_Armstrong_visit_to_Israel%2C_1959_%28997009327082505171%29.jpg/330px-Louis_Armstrong_visit_to_Israel%2C_1959_%28997009327082505171%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Louis_Armstrong_visit_to_Israel%2C_1959_%28997009327082505171%29.jpg/440px-Louis_Armstrong_visit_to_Israel%2C_1959_%28997009327082505171%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Armstrong_visit_to_Israel,_1959_(997009327082505171).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Louis Armstrong visit to Israel, April 1959</div></div></div>
<p>Armstrong was noted for his colorful and charismatic personality. His autobiography vexed some biographers and historians, as he had a habit of telling tales, particularly of his early childhood when he was less scrutinized, and his embellishments of his history often lack consistency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bergreen7_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-bergreen7-99">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to being an entertainer, Armstrong was a leading personality of the day. He was beloved by an American public that gave even the greatest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African American</a> performers little access beyond their public celebrity, and he was able to live a private life of access and privilege afforded to few other African Americans during that era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bergreen7_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-bergreen7-99">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>He generally remained politically neutral, which at times alienated him from members of the black community who looked to him to use his prominence with white America to become more of an outspoken figure during the <a href="/wiki/Civil_rights_movement" title="Civil rights movement">civil rights movement</a>. However, he did criticize <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/President_Eisenhower" title="President Eisenhower">President Eisenhower</a> for not acting forcefully enough on civil rights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bergreen7_99-2"><a href="#cite_note-bergreen7-99">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_problems">Health problems</span></h3>
<p>The trumpet is a notoriously hard instrument on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lips" title="Lips">lips</a>, and Armstrong suffered from lip damage over much of his life due to his aggressive style of playing and preference for narrow mouthpieces that would stay in place more easily, but which tended to dig into the soft flesh of his inner lip. During his 1930s European tour, he suffered an ulceration so severe that he had to stop playing entirely for a year. Eventually he took to using salves and creams on his lips and also cutting off scar tissue with a razor blade. By the 1950s, he was an official spokesman for Ansatz-Creme Lip Salve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>During a backstage meeting with trombonist Marshall Brown in 1959, Armstrong received the suggestion that he should go to a doctor and receive proper treatment for his lips instead of relying on home remedies, but he did not get around to arranging it until the final years of his life, by which point his health was failing and doctors considered surgery too risky.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>Also in 1959, Armstrong was hospitalized for <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a> while on tour in <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>. Doctors were concerned about his lungs and heart, but by June 26 he rallied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nicknames">Nicknames</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Armstrong-Alassio_(crop).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1385" data-file-width="2135" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Armstrong-Alassio_%28crop%29.jpg/220px-Armstrong-Alassio_%28crop%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Armstrong-Alassio_%28crop%29.jpg/330px-Armstrong-Alassio_%28crop%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Armstrong-Alassio_%28crop%29.jpg/440px-Armstrong-Alassio_%28crop%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Armstrong-Alassio_(crop).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Autograph of Armstrong on the muretto of <a href="/wiki/Alassio" title="Alassio">Alassio</a></div></div></div>
<p>The nicknames "Satchmo" and "Satch" are short for "Satchelmouth". The nickname has many possible origins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bergreen7_99-3"><a href="#cite_note-bergreen7-99">[98]</a></sup> The most common tale that biographers tell is the story of Armstrong as a young boy in New Orleans dancing for pennies. He scooped the coins off the street and stuck them into his mouth to prevent bigger children from stealing them. Someone dubbed him "satchel mouth" for his mouth acting as a satchel. Another tale is that because of his large mouth, he was nicknamed "satchel mouth" which was shortened to "Satchmo".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bergreen7_99-4"><a href="#cite_note-bergreen7-99">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>Early on he was also known as "Dipper", short for "Dippermouth", a reference to the piece <i>Dippermouth Blues</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup> and something of a riff on his unusual <a href="/wiki/Embouchure" title="Embouchure">embouchure</a>.
</p><p>The nickname "Pops" came from Armstrong's own tendency to forget people's names and simply call them "Pops" instead. The nickname was turned on Armstrong himself. It was used as the title of a 2010 biography of Armstrong by Terry Teachout.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bergreen7_99-5"><a href="#cite_note-bergreen7-99">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>After a competition at the Savoy, he was crowned and nicknamed "King Menelik", after the Emperor of Ethiopia, for slaying "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ofay" title="Ofay">ofay</a> jazz demons".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Race">Race</span></h3>
<p>Armstrong always celebrated his heritage as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">African American</a> man from a poor New Orleans neighborhood, and tried to avoid what he called "putting on airs." Many younger black musicians criticized Armstrong for playing in front of <a href="/wiki/Racial_segregation_in_the_United_States" title="Racial segregation in the United States">segregated</a> audiences, and for not taking a strong enough stand in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_civil_rights_movement" title="American civil rights movement">American civil rights movement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup> When he did speak out, it made national news, including his criticism of <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">President Dwight D. Eisenhower</a>, calling him "two-faced" and "gutless" because of his inaction during the <a href="/wiki/Little_Rock_Nine" title="Little Rock Nine">conflict</a> over school <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Desegregation" title="Desegregation">desegregation</a> in <a href="/wiki/Little_Rock,_Arkansas" title="Little Rock, Arkansas">Little Rock, Arkansas</a>, in 1957. As a protest, Armstrong canceled a planned tour of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> on behalf of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_Department" title="State Department">State Department</a> saying: "The way they're treating my people in the South, the government can go to hell" and that he could not represent his government abroad when it was in conflict with its own people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Louis_Armstrong,_Barring_Soviet_Tour,_Denounces_Eisenhower_and_Gov._Faubus_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Louis_Armstrong,_Barring_Soviet_Tour,_Denounces_Eisenhower_and_Gov._Faubus-106">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FBI" title="FBI">FBI</a> kept a file on Armstrong for his outspokenness about integration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>
<p>When asked about his religion, Armstrong answered that he was raised a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baptist" title="Baptist">Baptist</a>, always wore a <a href="/wiki/Star_of_David" title="Star of David">Star of David</a>, and was friends with the pope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gabbard_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gabbard-109">[108]</a></sup> He wore the Star of David in honor of the Karnoffsky family, who took him in as a child and lent him money to buy his first cornet. He was baptized a Catholic in the Sacred Heart of Jesus Church in New Orleans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gabbard_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gabbard-109">[108]</a></sup> and he met <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_XII" title="Pope Pius XII">Pope Pius XII</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_VI" title="Pope Paul VI">Pope Paul VI</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bergreen7_99-6"><a href="#cite_note-bergreen7-99">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_habits">Personal habits</span></h3>
<p>Armstrong was concerned with his health. He used <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Laxatives" title="Laxatives">laxatives</a> to control his weight, a practice he advocated both to acquaintances and in the diet plans he published under the title <i>Lose Weight the Satchmo Way</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bergreen7_99-7"><a href="#cite_note-bergreen7-99">[98]</a></sup> Armstrong's laxative of preference in his younger days was <a href="/wiki/Pluto_Water" title="Pluto Water">Pluto Water</a>, but when he discovered the herbal remedy <a href="/wiki/Senna_glycoside" title="Senna glycoside">Swiss Kriss</a>, he became an enthusiastic convert,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bergreen7_99-8"><a href="#cite_note-bergreen7-99">[98]</a></sup> extolling its virtues to anyone who would listen and passing out packets to everyone he encountered, including members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_Royal_Family" title="British Royal Family">British Royal Family</a>. (Armstrong also appeared in humorous, albeit risqué, cards that he had printed to send out to friends; the cards bore a picture of him sitting on a toilet—as viewed through a keyhole—with the slogan <i>"Satch says, 'Leave it all behind ya!'"</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeaveItAllBehindYa_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-LeaveItAllBehindYa-110">[109]</a></sup> The cards have sometimes been incorrectly described as ads for Swiss Kriss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup> In a live recording of "<a href="/wiki/Baby,_It%27s_Cold_Outside" title="Baby, It's Cold Outside">Baby, It's Cold Outside</a>" with <a href="/wiki/Velma_Middleton" title="Velma Middleton">Velma Middleton</a>, he changes the lyric from "Put another record on while I pour" to "Take some Swiss Kriss while I pour".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup> His laxative use began as a child when his mother would collect dandelions and peppergrass around the railroad tracks to give to her children for their health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>Armstrong was a heavy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marijuana" title="Marijuana">marijuana</a> smoker for much of his life and spent nine days in jail in 1930 after being arrested for drug possession outside a club. He described marijuana as "a thousand times better than whiskey".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>The concern with his health and weight was balanced by his love of food, reflected in such songs as "Cheesecake", "Cornet Chop Suey",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> though "Struttin' with Some Barbecue" was written about a fine-looking companion, not about food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup> He kept a strong connection throughout his life to the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana_Creole_cuisine" title="Louisiana Creole cuisine">cooking of New Orleans</a>, always signing his letters, "<a href="/wiki/Red_beans_and_rice" title="Red beans and rice">Red beans and ricely</a> yours ..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>A fan of Major League Baseball, he founded a team in New Orleans that was known as Raggedy Nine and transformed the team into his Armstrong's "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secret_Nine_Baseball" title="Secret Nine Baseball">Secret Nine Baseball</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Writings">Writings</span></h3>
<p>Armstrong's gregariousness extended to writing. On the road, he wrote constantly, sharing favorite themes of his life with correspondents around the world. He avidly typed or wrote on whatever stationery was at hand, recording instant takes on music, sex, food, childhood memories, his heavy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_marijuana" title="Medical marijuana">"medicinal" marijuana</a> use—and even his bowel movements, which he gleefully described.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_organizations">Social organizations</span></h3>
<p>Louis Armstrong was not, as is often claimed, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freemason" title="Freemason">Freemason</a>. Although he is usually listed as being a member of Montgomery Lodge No. 18 (Prince Hall) in New York, no such lodge has ever existed. However, Armstrong stated in his autobiography that he was a member of the <a href="/wiki/Knights_of_Pythias" title="Knights of Pythias">Knights of Pythias</a>, which although real is not a Masonic group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup>
