During the krewe's 1949 <a href="/wiki/Mardi_Gras_in_New_Orleans" title="Mardi Gras in New Orleans">Mardi Gras</a> parade, Armstrong presided as King of the <a href="/wiki/Zulu_Social_Aid_%26_Pleasure_Club" title="Zulu Social Aid &amp; Pleasure Club">Zulu Social Aid &amp; Pleasure Club</a>, for which he was featured on the cover of <a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)"><i>Time</i> magazine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Horn_playing_and_early_jazz">Horn playing and early jazz</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Selmer_Trumpet_given_by_King_George_V_to_Louis_Armstrong.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2440" data-file-width="3091" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Selmer_Trumpet_given_by_King_George_V_to_Louis_Armstrong.jpg/220px-Selmer_Trumpet_given_by_King_George_V_to_Louis_Armstrong.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Selmer_Trumpet_given_by_King_George_V_to_Louis_Armstrong.jpg/330px-Selmer_Trumpet_given_by_King_George_V_to_Louis_Armstrong.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Selmer_Trumpet_given_by_King_George_V_to_Louis_Armstrong.jpg/440px-Selmer_Trumpet_given_by_King_George_V_to_Louis_Armstrong.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Selmer_Trumpet_given_by_King_George_V_to_Louis_Armstrong.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Selmer trumpet, given as a gift by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_George_V" title="King George V">King George V</a> of the United Kingdom to Louis Armstrong in 1933</div></div></div>
<p>In his early years, Armstrong was best known for his virtuosity with the cornet and trumpet. Along with his "clarinet-like figurations and high notes in his cornet solos", he was also known for his "intense rhythmic 'swing', a complex conception involving ... accented upbeats, upbeat to downbeat slurring, and complementary relations among rhythmic patterns."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup> The most lauded recordings on which Armstrong plays trumpet include the <a href="/wiki/Louis_Armstrong_Hot_Five_and_Hot_Seven_Sessions" title="Louis Armstrong Hot Five and Hot Seven Sessions">Hot Five and Hot Seven sessions</a>, as well as those of the <a href="/wiki/Red_Onion_Jazz_Babies" title="Red Onion Jazz Babies">Red Onion Jazz Babies</a>. Armstrong's improvisations, while unconventionally sophisticated for that era, were also subtle and highly melodic. The solo that Armstrong plays during the song "<a href="/wiki/Potato_Head_Blues" title="Potato Head Blues">Potato Head Blues</a>" has long been considered his best solo of that series.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bergreen7_99-9"><a href="#cite_note-bergreen7-99">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>Prior to Armstrong, most collective ensemble playing in jazz, along with its occasional solos, simply varied the melodies of the songs. Armstrong was virtually the first to create significant variations based on the chord harmonies of the songs instead of merely on the melodies. This opened a rich field for creation and improvisation, and significantly changed the music into a soloist's art form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bergreen7_99-10"><a href="#cite_note-bergreen7-99">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>Often, Armstrong re-composed pop-tunes he played, simply with variations that made them more compelling to jazz listeners of the era. At the same time, however, his oeuvre includes many original melodies, creative leaps, and relaxed or driving rhythms. Armstrong's playing technique, honed by constant practice, extended the range, tone and capabilities of the trumpet. In his records, Armstrong almost single-handedly created the role of the jazz soloist, taking what had been essentially a collective folk music and turning it into an art form with tremendous possibilities for individual expression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bergreen7_99-11"><a href="#cite_note-bergreen7-99">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>Armstrong was one of the first artists to use recordings of his performances to improve himself. Armstrong was an avid audiophile. He had a large collection of recordings, including reel-to-reel tapes, which he took on the road with him in a trunk during his later career. He enjoyed listening to his own recordings, and comparing his performances musically. In the den of his home, he had the latest audio equipment and would sometimes rehearse and record along with his older recordings or the radio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocal_popularity">Vocal popularity</span></h3>
<p>As his music progressed and popularity grew, his singing also became very important. Armstrong was not the first to record scat singing, but he was masterful at it and helped popularize it with the first recording on which he scatted, "<a href="/wiki/Heebie_Jeebies_(composition)" title="Heebie Jeebies (composition)">Heebie Jeebies</a>". At a recording session for <a href="/wiki/Okeh_Records" title="Okeh Records">Okeh Records</a>, when the sheet music supposedly fell on the floor and the music began before he could pick up the pages, Armstrong simply started singing nonsense syllables while Okeh president E.A. Fearn, who was at the session, kept telling him to continue. Armstrong did, thinking the track would be discarded, but that was the version that was pressed to disc, sold, and became an unexpected hit. Although the story was thought to be apocryphal, Armstrong himself confirmed it in at least one interview as well as in his memoirs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[124]</a></sup> On a later recording, Armstrong also sang out "I done forgot the words" in the middle of recording "I'm A Ding Dong Daddy From Dumas".
</p><p>Such records were hits and scat singing became a major part of his performances. Long before this, however, Armstrong was playing around with his vocals, shortening and lengthening phrases, interjecting improvisations, using his voice as creatively as his trumpet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bergreen7_99-12"><a href="#cite_note-bergreen7-99">[98]</a></sup> Armstrong once told <a href="/wiki/Cab_Calloway" title="Cab Calloway">Cab Calloway</a> that his scat style was derived "from the Jews <i>rockin</i>", an Orthodox Jewish style of chanting during prayer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Composing">Composing</span></h3>
<p>Armstrong was a gifted composer who wrote more than fifty songs, some of which have become <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jazz_standards" title="Jazz standards">jazz standards</a> (e.g. "Gully Low Blues", "Potato Head Blues" and "Swing That Music").
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Colleagues_and_followers">Colleagues and followers</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Satchmo_Messuhallissa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="602" data-file-width="900" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Satchmo_Messuhallissa.jpg/220px-Satchmo_Messuhallissa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Satchmo_Messuhallissa.jpg/330px-Satchmo_Messuhallissa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Satchmo_Messuhallissa.jpg/440px-Satchmo_Messuhallissa.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Satchmo_Messuhallissa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>With <a href="/wiki/Jack_Teagarden" title="Jack Teagarden">Jack Teagarden</a> (left) and <a href="/wiki/Barney_Bigard" title="Barney Bigard">Barney Bigard</a> (right), Armstrong plays the trumpet in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Helsinki,_Finland" title="Helsinki, Finland">Helsinki, Finland</a>, October 1949.</div></div></div>
<p>During his long career he played and sang with some of the most important instrumentalists and vocalists of the time; among them were Bing Crosby, <a href="/wiki/Duke_Ellington" title="Duke Ellington">Duke Ellington</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fletcher_Henderson" title="Fletcher Henderson">Fletcher Henderson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Earl_Hines" title="Earl Hines">Earl Hines</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jimmie_Rodgers_(country_singer)" title="Jimmie Rodgers (country singer)">Jimmie Rodgers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bessie_Smith" title="Bessie Smith">Bessie Smith</a> and perhaps most famously <a href="/wiki/Ella_Fitzgerald" title="Ella Fitzgerald">Ella Fitzgerald</a>. His influence upon Crosby is particularly important with regard to the subsequent development of popular music: Crosby admired and copied Armstrong, as is evident on many of his early recordings, notably "Just One More Chance" (1931).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bergreen7_99-13"><a href="#cite_note-bergreen7-99">[98]</a></sup> The <i>New Grove Dictionary of Jazz</i> describes Crosby's debt to Armstrong in precise detail, although it does not acknowledge Armstrong by name:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Crosby ... was important in introducing into the mainstream of popular singing an Afro-American concept of song as a lyrical extension of speech ... His techniques—easing the weight of the breath on the vocal cords, passing into a <a href="/wiki/Head_voice" title="Head voice">head voice</a> at a low register, using forward production to aid distinct <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enunciation" title="Enunciation">enunciation</a>, singing on consonants (a practice of black singers), and making discreet use of <a href="/wiki/Appoggiatura" title="Appoggiatura">appoggiaturas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mordent" title="Mordent">mordents</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Slur_(music)" title="Slur (music)">slurs</a> to emphasize the text—were emulated by nearly all later popular singers.</p></blockquote>
<p>Armstrong recorded two albums with Ella Fitzgerald: <i><a href="/wiki/Ella_and_Louis" title="Ella and Louis">Ella and Louis</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Ella_and_Louis_Again" title="Ella and Louis Again">Ella and Louis Again</a></i> for <a href="/wiki/Verve_Records" title="Verve Records">Verve Records</a>, with the sessions featuring the backing musicianship of the <a href="/wiki/Oscar_Peterson" title="Oscar Peterson">Oscar Peterson</a> Trio and drummers <a href="/wiki/Buddy_Rich" title="Buddy Rich">Buddy Rich</a> (on the first album), and <a href="/wiki/Louie_Bellson" title="Louie Bellson">Louie Bellson</a> (on the second). <a href="/wiki/Norman_Granz" title="Norman Granz">Norman Granz</a> then had the vision for Ella and Louis to record <i><a href="/wiki/Porgy_and_Bess_(Ella_Fitzgerald_and_Louis_Armstrong_album)" title="Porgy and Bess (Ella Fitzgerald and Louis Armstrong album)">Porgy and Bess</a></i>.
</p><p>His recordings for <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Records" title="Columbia Records">Columbia Records</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Louis_Armstrong_Plays_W.C._Handy" title="Louis Armstrong Plays W.C. Handy">Louis Armstrong Plays W.C. Handy</a></i> (1954) and <i>Satch Plays Fats</i> (all <a href="/wiki/Fats_Waller" title="Fats Waller">Fats Waller</a> tunes) (1955) were both being considered masterpieces, as well as moderately well selling. In 1961 the All Stars participated in two albums—<i>The Great Summit</i> and <i>The Great Reunion</i> (now together as a single disc) with <a href="/wiki/Duke_Ellington" title="Duke Ellington">Duke Ellington</a>. The albums feature many of Ellington's most famous compositions (as well as two exclusive cuts) with Duke sitting in on piano. His participation in <a href="/wiki/Dave_Brubeck" title="Dave Brubeck">Dave Brubeck</a>'s high-concept jazz musical <i><a href="/wiki/The_Real_Ambassadors" title="The Real Ambassadors">The Real Ambassadors</a></i> (1963) was critically acclaimed, and features "Summer Song", one of Armstrong's most popular vocal efforts.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Armstrong_NYWTS_4.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2457" decoding="async" height="208" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Louis_Armstrong_NYWTS_4.jpg/170px-Louis_Armstrong_NYWTS_4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Louis_Armstrong_NYWTS_4.jpg/255px-Louis_Armstrong_NYWTS_4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Louis_Armstrong_NYWTS_4.jpg/340px-Louis_Armstrong_NYWTS_4.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Armstrong_NYWTS_4.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Louis Armstrong in 1966</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/List_of_Billboard_Hot_100_number_ones_of_1964" title="List of Billboard Hot 100 number ones of 1964">In the week beginning May 9, 1964</a>, his recording of the song "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hello_Dolly_(song)" title="Hello Dolly (song)">Hello, Dolly</a>" went to number one. An <a href="/wiki/Hello,_Dolly!_(Louis_Armstrong_album)" title="Hello, Dolly! (Louis Armstrong album)">album of the same title</a> was quickly created around the song, and also shot to number one (knocking <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">The Beatles</a> off the top of the chart). The album sold very well for the rest of the year, quickly going "Gold" (500,000). His performance of "Hello Dolly" won for best male pop vocal performance at the 1964 <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Awards" title="Grammy Awards">Grammy Awards</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hits_and_later_career">Hits and later career</span></h3>
<p>Armstrong had nineteen "Top Ten" records<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-billboard.com_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-billboard.com-128">[127]</a></sup> including "<a href="/wiki/Stardust_(1927_song)" title="Stardust (1927 song)">Stardust</a>", "<a href="/wiki/What_a_Wonderful_World" title="What a Wonderful World">What a Wonderful World</a>", "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/When_The_Saints_Go_Marching_In" title="When The Saints Go Marching In">When The Saints Go Marching In</a>", "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dream_a_Little_Dream_of_Me_(song)" title="Dream a Little Dream of Me (song)">Dream a Little Dream of Me</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Ain%27t_Misbehavin%27_(song)" title="Ain't Misbehavin' (song)">Ain't Misbehavin'</a>", "<a href="/wiki/You_Rascal_You" title="You Rascal You">You Rascal You</a>", and "<a href="/wiki/Stompin%27_at_the_Savoy" title="Stompin' at the Savoy">Stompin' at the Savoy</a>". "<a href="/wiki/We_Have_All_the_Time_in_the_World" title="We Have All the Time in the World">We Have All the Time in the World</a>" was featured on the soundtrack of the <a href="/wiki/James_Bond" title="James Bond">James Bond</a> film <i><a href="/wiki/On_Her_Majesty%27s_Secret_Service_(film)" title="On Her Majesty's Secret Service (film)">On Her Majesty's Secret Service</a></i>, and enjoyed renewed popularity in the UK in 1994 when it featured on a <a href="/wiki/Guinness" title="Guinness">Guinness</a> advertisement. It reached number 3 in the charts on being re-released.
</p><p>In 1964, Armstrong knocked <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">The Beatles</a> off the top of the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100" title="Billboard Hot 100"><i>Billboard</i> Hot 100</a> chart with "<a href="/wiki/Hello,_Dolly!_(song)" title="Hello, Dolly! (song)">Hello, Dolly!</a>", which gave the 63-year-old performer a U.S. record as the oldest artist to have a number one song. His 1964 song "Bout Time" was later featured in the film <i><a href="/wiki/Bewitched_(2005_film)" title="Bewitched (2005 film)">Bewitched</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bergreen7_99-14"><a href="#cite_note-bergreen7-99">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 1968, he appeared with Lara Saint Paul on the Italian <a href="/wiki/RAI" title="RAI">RAI</a> television channel where he performed "Grassa e Bella", a track he sang in Italian for the Italian market and C.D.I. label.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1968, Armstrong scored one last popular hit in the United Kingdom with "<a href="/wiki/What_a_Wonderful_World" title="What a Wonderful World">What a Wonderful World</a>", which topped the British charts for a month. Armstrong appeared on the October 28, 1970, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Johnny_Cash_Show_(TV_series)" title="The Johnny Cash Show (TV series)">Johnny Cash Show</a></i>, where he sang <a href="/wiki/Nat_King_Cole" title="Nat King Cole">Nat King Cole</a>'s hit "<a href="/wiki/Ramblin%27_Rose" title="Ramblin' Rose">Ramblin' Rose</a>" and joined Cash to re-create his performance backing Jimmie Rodgers on "Blue Yodel No. 9".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stylistic_range">Stylistic range</span></h3>
<p>Armstrong enjoyed many types of music, from blues to the arrangements of <a href="/wiki/Guy_Lombardo" title="Guy Lombardo">Guy Lombardo</a>, to Latin American folksongs, to classical symphonies and <a href="/wiki/Opera" title="Opera">opera</a>. He incorporated influences from all these sources into his performances, sometimes to the bewilderment of fans who wanted him to stay in convenient narrow categories. Armstrong was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame" title="Rock and Roll Hall of Fame">Rock and Roll Hall of Fame</a> as an <i>early influence</i>. Some of his solos from the 1950s, such as the <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_roll" title="Rock and roll">hard rocking</a> version of "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Louis_Blues_(song)" title="St. Louis Blues (song)">St. Louis Blues</a>" from the <i>WC Handy</i> album, show that the influence went in both directions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bergreen7_99-15"><a href="#cite_note-bergreen7-99">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Film.2C_television.2C_and_radio"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Film,_television,_and_radio">Film, television, and radio</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Louis_Armstrong_filmography" title="Louis Armstrong filmography">Louis Armstrong filmography</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Armstrong_and_Grace_Kelly_on_the_set_of_%22High_Society%22,_1956.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3362" data-file-width="2886" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Louis_Armstrong_and_Grace_Kelly_on_the_set_of_%22High_Society%22%2C_1956.jpg/180px-Louis_Armstrong_and_Grace_Kelly_on_the_set_of_%22High_Society%22%2C_1956.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Louis_Armstrong_and_Grace_Kelly_on_the_set_of_%22High_Society%22%2C_1956.jpg/270px-Louis_Armstrong_and_Grace_Kelly_on_the_set_of_%22High_Society%22%2C_1956.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Louis_Armstrong_and_Grace_Kelly_on_the_set_of_%22High_Society%22%2C_1956.jpg/360px-Louis_Armstrong_and_Grace_Kelly_on_the_set_of_%22High_Society%22%2C_1956.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Louis_Armstrong_and_Grace_Kelly_on_the_set_of_%22High_Society%22,_1956.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Armstrong entertains <a href="/wiki/Grace_Kelly" title="Grace Kelly">Grace Kelly</a> on the set of <i>High Society</i> in 1956.</div></div></div>
<p>Armstrong appeared in more than a dozen Hollywood films, usually playing a bandleader or musician. His most familiar role was as the bandleader <i>cum</i> narrator in the 1956 musical <i><a href="/wiki/High_Society_(1956_film)" title="High Society (1956 film)">High Society</a></i> starring Bing Crosby, <a href="/wiki/Grace_Kelly" title="Grace Kelly">Grace Kelly</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frank_Sinatra" title="Frank Sinatra">Frank Sinatra</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Celeste_Holm" title="Celeste Holm">Celeste Holm</a>. He appears throughout the film, also sings the title song as well as performs a duet with Crosby, "<a href="/wiki/Now_You_Has_Jazz" title="Now You Has Jazz">Now You Has Jazz</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup> In 1947, he played himself in the movie <i><a href="/wiki/New_Orleans_(1947_film)" title="New Orleans (1947 film)">New Orleans</a></i> opposite Billie Holiday, which chronicled the demise of the <a href="/wiki/Storyville,_New_Orleans#Closure" title="Storyville, New Orleans">Storyville district</a> and the ensuing exodus of musicians from New Orleans to Chicago. In the 1959 film <i><a href="/wiki/The_Five_Pennies" title="The Five Pennies">The Five Pennies</a></i> he played himself, sang, and played several classic numbers. With <a href="/wiki/Danny_Kaye" title="Danny Kaye">Danny Kaye</a> he performed a duet of "When the Saints Go Marching In" during which Kaye impersonated Armstrong. He had a part in the film alongside James Stewart in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Glenn_Miller_Story" title="The Glenn Miller Story">The Glenn Miller Story</a></i>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hello,_Dolly!12.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="356" data-file-width="843" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Hello%2C_Dolly%2112.jpg/260px-Hello%2C_Dolly%2112.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Hello%2C_Dolly%2112.jpg/390px-Hello%2C_Dolly%2112.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Hello%2C_Dolly%2112.jpg/520px-Hello%2C_Dolly%2112.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hello,_Dolly!12.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Armstrong alongside <a href="/wiki/Barbra_Streisand" title="Barbra Streisand">Barbra Streisand</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Hello,_Dolly!_(film)" title="Hello, Dolly! (film)">Hello, Dolly!</a></i> (1969)</div></div></div>
<p>In 1937, Armstrong was the first African American to host a <a href="/wiki/The_Fleischmann%27s_Yeast_Hour#Guests" title="The Fleischmann's Yeast Hour">nationally broadcast radio show</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LAHM-still_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-LAHM-still-131">[130]</a></sup> In 1969, he had a cameo role in <a href="/wiki/Gene_Kelly" title="Gene Kelly">Gene Kelly</a>'s film version of <i><a href="/wiki/Hello,_Dolly!_(film)" title="Hello, Dolly! (film)">Hello, Dolly!</a></i> as the bandleader Louis. He sang the title song with actress <a href="/wiki/Barbra_Streisand" title="Barbra Streisand">Barbra Streisand</a>. His solo recording of "<a href="/wiki/Hello,_Dolly!_(song)" title="Hello, Dolly! (song)">Hello, Dolly!</a>" is one of his most recognizable performances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bergreen7_99-16"><a href="#cite_note-bergreen7-99">[98]</a></sup> He was heard on such radio programs as <i>The Story of Swing</i> (1937) and <i>This Is Jazz</i> (1947), and he also made television appearances, especially in the 1950s and 1960s, including appearances on <i><a href="/wiki/The_Tonight_Show_Starring_Johnny_Carson" title="The Tonight Show Starring Johnny Carson">The Tonight Show Starring Johnny Carson</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bergreen7_99-17"><a href="#cite_note-bergreen7-99">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>Argentine writer <a href="/wiki/Julio_Cort%C3%A1zar" title="Julio Cortázar">Julio Cortázar</a>, a self-described Armstrong admirer, asserted that a 1952 Louis Armstrong concert at the <a href="/wiki/Th%C3%A9%C3%A2tre_des_Champs-%C3%89lys%C3%A9es" title="Théâtre des Champs-Élysées">Théâtre des Champs-Élysées</a> in Paris played a significant role in inspiring him to create the fictional creatures called <a href="/wiki/Cronopio_(literature)" title="Cronopio (literature)">Cronopios</a> that are the subject of a number of Cortázar's short stories. Cortázar once called Armstrong himself "Grandísimo Cronopio" (The Great Cronopio).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bergreen7_99-18"><a href="#cite_note-bergreen7-99">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is a pivotal scene in <i><a href="/wiki/Stardust_Memories" title="Stardust Memories">Stardust Memories</a></i> (1980) in which <a href="/wiki/Woody_Allen" title="Woody Allen">Woody Allen</a> is overwhelmed by a recording of Armstrong's "<a href="/wiki/Stardust_(1927_song)" title="Stardust (1927 song)">Stardust</a>" and experiences a nostalgic epiphany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death">Death</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Satchmo%27s_place.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="3872" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Satchmo%27s_place.jpg/220px-Satchmo%27s_place.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Satchmo%27s_place.jpg/330px-Satchmo%27s_place.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Satchmo%27s_place.jpg/440px-Satchmo%27s_place.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Satchmo%27s_place.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Flushing Cemetery resting place of Louis 'Satchmo' Armstrong</div></div></div>
<p>Against his doctor's advice, Armstrong played a two-week engagement in March 1971 at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waldorf-Astoria" title="Waldorf-Astoria">Waldorf-Astoria</a>'s Empire Room. At the end of it, he was hospitalized for a <a href="/wiki/Myocardial_infarction" title="Myocardial infarction">heart attack</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup> He was released from the hospital in May, and quickly resumed practicing his trumpet playing. Still hoping to get back on the road, Armstrong died of a heart attack in his sleep on July 6, 1971, two days after celebrating his alleged 71st birthday,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup> and a month before his actual 70th birthday.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-birthdate_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-birthdate-11">[a]</a></sup> He was residing in <a href="/wiki/Corona,_Queens" title="Corona, Queens">Corona, Queens</a>, New York City, at the time of his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[135]</a></sup> He was interred in <a href="/wiki/Flushing_Cemetery" title="Flushing Cemetery">Flushing Cemetery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flushing,_Queens" title="Flushing, Queens">Flushing</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Queens" title="Queens">Queens</a>, New York City.
His honorary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pallbearers" title="Pallbearers">pallbearers</a> included Bing Crosby, <a href="/wiki/Ella_Fitzgerald" title="Ella Fitzgerald">Ella Fitzgerald</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dizzy_Gillespie" title="Dizzy Gillespie">Dizzy Gillespie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pearl_Bailey" title="Pearl Bailey">Pearl Bailey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Count_Basie" title="Count Basie">Count Basie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harry_James" title="Harry James">Harry James</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frank_Sinatra" title="Frank Sinatra">Frank Sinatra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ed_Sullivan" title="Ed Sullivan">Ed Sullivan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Earl_Wilson_(columnist)" title="Earl Wilson (columnist)">Earl Wilson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alan_King" title="Alan King">Alan King</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Carson" title="Johnny Carson">Johnny Carson</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_Frost" title="David Frost">David Frost</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[136]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Peggy_Lee" title="Peggy Lee">Peggy Lee</a> sang <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Lord%27s_Prayer" title="The Lord's Prayer">The Lord's Prayer</a> at the services while <a href="/wiki/Al_Hibbler" title="Al Hibbler">Al Hibbler</a> sang "<a href="/wiki/Nobody_Knows_the_Trouble_I%27ve_Seen" title="Nobody Knows the Trouble I've Seen">Nobody Knows the Trouble I've Seen</a>" and Fred Robbins, a long-time friend, gave the eulogy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[137]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards_and_honors">Awards and honors</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammy_Awards">Grammy Awards</span></h3>
<p>Armstrong was posthumously awarded the <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Lifetime_Achievement_Award" title="Grammy Lifetime Achievement Award">Grammy Lifetime Achievement Award</a> in 1972 by the Academy of Recording Arts and Sciences. This Special Merit Award is presented by vote of the Recording Academy's National Trustees to performers who, during their lifetimes, have made creative contributions of outstanding artistic significance to the field of recording.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[138]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammy_Hall_of_Fame">Grammy Hall of Fame</span></h3>
<p>Recordings of Armstrong were inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Grammy_Hall_of_Fame" title="Grammy Hall of Fame">Grammy Hall of Fame</a>, which is a special Grammy award established in 1973 to honor recordings that are at least 25 years old, and that have "qualitative or historical significance".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[140]</a></sup>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame">Rock and Roll Hall of Fame</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Rock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame" title="Rock and Roll Hall of Fame">Rock and Roll Hall of Fame</a> listed Armstrong's <i>West End Blues</i> on the list of 500 songs that shaped Rock and Roll.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Film_honors">Film honors</span></h3>
<p>In 1999 Armstrong was nominated for inclusion in the <a href="/wiki/American_Film_Institute" title="American Film Institute">American Film Institute</a>'s <a href="/wiki/AFI%27s_100_Years...100_Stars" title="AFI's 100 Years...100 Stars">100 Years ... 100 Stars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[143]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<p>The influence of Armstrong on the development of jazz is virtually immeasurable. His irrepressible personality both as a performer and as a public figure was so strong that to some it sometimes overshadowed his contributions as a musician and singer.
</p><p>As a virtuoso trumpet player, Armstrong had a unique tone and an extraordinary talent for melodic <a href="/wiki/Improvisation" title="Improvisation">improvisation</a>. Through his playing, the trumpet emerged as a solo instrument in jazz and is used widely today. Additionally, jazz itself was transformed from a collectively improvised folk music to a soloist's serious art form largely through his influence. He was a masterful accompanist and ensemble player in addition to his extraordinary skills as a soloist. With his innovations, he raised the bar musically for all who came after him.
</p><p>Though Armstrong is widely recognized as a pioneer of scat singing, <a href="/wiki/Ethel_Waters" title="Ethel Waters">Ethel Waters</a> precedes his scatting on record in the 1930s according to <a href="/wiki/Gary_Giddins" title="Gary Giddins">Gary Giddins</a> and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[144]</a></sup> Billie Holiday and Frank Sinatra are just two singers who were greatly indebted to him. Holiday said that she always wanted <a href="/wiki/Bessie_Smith" title="Bessie Smith">Bessie Smith</a>'s 'big' sound and Armstrong's feeling in her singing. Even special musicians like Duke Ellington have praised Armstrong through strong testimonials. Duke Ellington, <a href="/wiki/DownBeat" title="DownBeat">DownBeat</a> magazine in 1971, said, "If anybody was a master, it was Louis Armstrong. He was and will continue to be the embodiment of jazz."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[145]</a></sup> In 1950, <a href="/wiki/Bing_Crosby" title="Bing Crosby">Bing Crosby</a>, the most successful vocalist of the first half of the 20th century, said, "He is the beginning and the end of music in America."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the summer of 2001, in commemoration of the centennial of Armstrong's birth, New Orleans's main airport was renamed <a href="/wiki/Louis_Armstrong_New_Orleans_International_Airport" title="Louis Armstrong New Orleans International Airport">Louis Armstrong New Orleans International Airport</a>. The entrance to the airport's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Former_terminal_of_New_Orleans_International_Airport" title="Former terminal of New Orleans International Airport">former terminal building</a> houses a statue depicting Armstrong playing his cornet.
</p><p>In 2002, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_Armstrong_and_his_Hot_Five" title="Louis Armstrong and his Hot Five">Louis Armstrong's Hot Five and Hot Seven</a> recordings (1925–1928) were preserved in the United States National Recording Registry, a registry of <a href="/wiki/Sound_recording_and_reproduction" title="Sound recording and reproduction">recordings</a> selected yearly by the <a href="/wiki/National_Recording_Preservation_Board" title="National Recording Preservation Board">National Recording Preservation Board</a> for preservation in the <a href="/wiki/National_Recording_Registry" title="National Recording Registry">National Recording Registry</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/US_Open_(tennis)" title="US Open (tennis)">US Open</a> tennis tournament's former main stadium was named <a href="/wiki/Louis_Armstrong_Stadium" title="Louis Armstrong Stadium">Louis Armstrong Stadium</a> in honor of Armstrong who had lived a few blocks from the site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Congo_Square" title="Congo Square">Congo Square</a></i> was a common gathering place for African-Americans in New Orleans for dancing and performing music. The park where Congo Square is located was later renamed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louis_Armstrong_Park" title="Louis Armstrong Park">Louis Armstrong Park</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[149]</a></sup> Dedicated in April 1980, the park includes a 12-foot (3.7 m) statue of Armstrong, trumpet in hand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[150]</a></sup>
</p><p>The house where Armstrong lived for almost 28 years was declared a <a href="/wiki/National_Historic_Landmark" title="National Historic Landmark">National Historic Landmark</a> in 1977 and is now a museum. The <a href="/wiki/Louis_Armstrong_House" title="Louis Armstrong House">Louis Armstrong House Museum</a>, at 34–56 107th Street between 34th and 37th avenues in <a href="/wiki/Corona,_Queens" title="Corona, Queens">Corona, Queens</a>, presents concerts and educational programs, operates as a historic house museum and makes materials in its archives of writings, books, recordings and memorabilia available to the public for research. The museum is operated by the <a href="/wiki/Queens_College,_City_University_of_New_York" title="Queens College, City University of New York">Queens College, City University of New York</a>, following the dictates of Lucille Armstrong's will. The museum opened to the public on October 15, 2003. A new visitors center is planned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-visitorscenter_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-visitorscenter-152">[151]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to literary critic Harold Bloom, "The two great American contributions to the world's art, in the end, are Walt Whitman and, after him, Armstrong and jazz ... If I had to choose between the two, ultimately, I wouldn't. I would say that the genius of this nation at its best is indeed Walt Whitman and Louis Armstrong."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[152]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-InHisOwnWords-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-InHisOwnWords_22-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFArmstrong2001">Armstrong, Louis (2001).  Brothers, Thomas (ed.). <i>Louis Armstrong, in His Own Words: Selected Writings</i> (1st Oxford University Press paperback ed.). Oxford: Oxford University Press. p. 8. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-514046-0" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-514046-0"><bdi>978-0-19-514046-0</bdi></a>. <q>I had a long time admiration for the Jewish people. Especially with their long time of courage, taking so much abuse for so long. I was only seven years old, but I could easily see the ungodly treatment that the white folks were handing the poor Jewish family whom I worked for. It dawned on me, how drastically. Even "my race", the Negroes, the way that I saw it, they were having a little better break than the Jewish people, with jobs a plenty around. Of course, we can understand all the situations and handicaps that was going on, but to me we were better off than the Jewish people.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Louis+Armstrong%2C+in+His+Own+Words%3A+Selected+Writings&amp;rft.place=Oxford&amp;rft.pages=8&amp;rft.edition=1st+Oxford+University+Press+paperback&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2001&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-514046-0&amp;rft.aulast=Armstrong&amp;rft.aufirst=Louis&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ALouis+Armstrong"></span></span>
